Three Excuses for Dropping Order Between Missed and Current Prayers
Explains three excuses that cancel the requirement of order for making up missed prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three excuses that drop the requirement of order (tartib) between missed and current prayers, and explain the evidence for each according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Order between missed and current prayers is wajib but dropped for narrow time, forgetfulness, or six missed prayers – to preserve current prayer's perfection and avoid hardship.

The Hanafi school requires order (tartib) when making up missed prayers, but recognizes real-world constraints. This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi refines the rule by identifying three specific excuses that lift the order requirement, balancing textual evidence (Qur'an and mutawatir reports) with practical ease. We will unpack each excuse and the proofs behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Excuses
From the passage, extract the three reasons that cancel the obligation of order: (1) narrow preferred time, (2) forgetfulness, (3) when missed prayers reach six or more. These are the core exceptions.
Each excuse has its own textual basis and conditions.
2
Analyze the Narrow Time Excuse and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Focus on the first excuse: narrow time (ḍīq al-waqt). Note the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf (consider absolute time) and Imam Muhammad (consider preferred time). Identify which view is relied upon and why, following the commentary's reasoning about mutawatir vs. solitary hadith.
The preferred time is the earliest part; performing prayer later is makruh tanzīh.
3
Understand the Forgetfulness Excuse
The second excuse: genuine forgetfulness (nisyān). The passage argues that if one is unaware of the missed prayer at the time of the current prayer, order is not required because the missed prayer's time was not present in awareness, and Allah does not burden beyond capacity.
This applies even if one remembers later; order is not revived.
4
Examine the Six Missed Prayers Excuse
The third excuse: when the number of missed prayers (actual or deemed) becomes six or more, order is dropped to avoid hardship. The passage clarifies that the exit of the sixth prayer's time triggers this, and that witr is not counted in the six.
The principle: multiplicity beyond five enters the realm of repetition, lifting order.
5
Review the Proofs and Thamarah al-Khilaf
For each excuse, note the proofs: for narrow time, the Qur'anic command for complete prayer and mutawatir reports vs. solitary hadith of order; for forgetfulness, the Quranic principle of ease; for six, the general principle of repelling hardship. Also note the practical fruit of the intra-Hanafi disagreement (cutting vs. continuing).
The relied-upon view is Imam Muhammad's, as supported by al-Muntaqa.

الثالث إذا صارت الفوائت الحقيقية أو الحكمية (ستاً) لأنه لو وجب الترتيب فيها لوقعوا في حرج عظيم وهو مدفوع بالنص والمعتبر خروج وقت السادسة في الصحيح لأن الكثرة بالدخول في حد التكرار وروي بدخول وقت السادسة لأن الزائد على الخمس في حكم التكرار ومثال الكثرة الحكمية سنذكرها بصلاته خمساً متذكراً فائتة لم يقضها حتى خرج وقت السادسة من المؤديات متذكراً، وكما سقط الترتيب فيما بين والحاضرة سقط فيما بين أنفسها على الأصح وقيدناها بكونها ستاً (غير الوتر فإنه لا يعد
The preferred (time): Because observing the order would cause the current prayer to occur deficiently, thus changing the ruling of the Book, so order is dropped due to narrow preferred time, and it does not return after its exit (in the most correct view). Example: if he busies himself with making up Dhuhr, the Asr or part of it would occur in the changed time, so order is dropped in the most correct view. Consideration is given to narrowness at commencement. If he starts the current prayer remembering the missed one, then that is because the end of the time is for the current prayer by widely transmitted reports and texts, while the time of remembrance for the missed prayer is established by the previous hadith. For in some of its narrations: 'That is its time,' which indicates obligation of order, but it is described as a solitary hadith, and it is only obligatory to act upon it if it does not entail abandoning the text; otherwise, it would entail abrogating the Book by it, which is not permissible, as in the commentary. His statement: (then) i.e. when time becomes narrow. His statement: (and he does not act upon it) i.e. upon the well-known, which is the previous hadith, for it loses the obligation of order. His statement: (by spacious time) the ba is for reason; in another copy: lam. His statement: (by narrow time) is connected to his saying 'wasting', and the ba is for reason. If he advanced the missed prayer and it was not a disliked time, it is valid, but he sins for causing the current prayer to be lost without justification, becoming like one who busies himself with supererogatory prayer when time is narrow; unlike when time is spacious and he advanced the current prayer, where it is not valid because he performed it before its time established by the hadith while it was possible to combine both. His statement: (the preferred) was not mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah, so there was disagreement among the mashayikh. Al-Tahawi attributed consideration of the absolute time to both (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), and consideration of the preferred time to Muhammad. In al-Muhit, Muhammad's view was preferred; also in al-Zahiriyyah based on al-Muntaqa that if he began Asr at its earliest time, forgetting Dhuhr, then the sun became red, then he remembered Dhuhr, he continues with Asr. He said: This is explicit that consideration is for the preferred time. Then the disagreement among the mashayikh ended because the issue was not mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah, and when it is established in another transmission, one must adhere to it. The fruit of the difference appears if he began Asr while forgetting Dhuhr, then remembered it at a time that if he busies himself with it, Asr would occur in the disliked time: he cuts off Asr according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and prays Dhuhr, but according to him (Muhammad) he continues with Asr then prays Dhuhr after sunset. Al-Sayyid mentioned this fruit from Muskihin. His statement: 'then the ruling of the Book changes' refers to His saying: 'Indeed prayer has been decreed upon the believers at fixed times' [al-Nisa' 4:103]. Changing the ruling of the Book is diminishing the current prayer by performing it in the disliked time, as in the commentary. For the mentioned verse, like His saying 'Establish prayer', indicates performing the obligatory with perfection, as that is what is required by law. The interpretation of narrow time is that the remaining time does not accommodate both the current and missed prayers in reality, not based on his assumption. If one who owes Isha' finds Fajr time narrow and prays Fajr, then it becomes clear that time was spacious, Fajr is invalidated. Then he looks: if the time accommodates both such that he can sit in Fajr for the tashahhud before sunrise after praying Isha', he prays Isha' then repeats Fajr; if there is no such spaciousness, he only repeats Fajr. And so on, time after time. And if he prolonged it until time became narrow, it is not valid unless he cuts it off then begins it. And if he begins forgetfully, the case being as such, then remembers at narrow time, the current prayer is permissible. If there are multiple missed prayers and the time accommodates only some along with the current prayer, order is dropped in the most correct view as we indicated, because directing to this particular missed prayer is not more appropriate than another, as in al-Fath. (And) the second: forgetfulness, because he is unable to perform; forgetfulness is not burdened by Allah except within capacity; and because its time did not come into existence due to not remembering, so it did not coincide with the current prayer. (And) the third: when the missed prayers, real or deemed, reach six, because if order were required, they would fall into great hardship, which is repelled by the text. What is considered is the exit of the time of the sixth in the correct view, because multiplicity enters into the limit of repetition. It is narrated by the entry of the time of the sixth because what exceeds five is in the ruling of repetition. An example of deemed multiplicity: we will mention it: if he prayed five while remembering a missed prayer and did not make it up until the time of the sixth of the current prayers exited while remembering. And just as order between them and the current prayer is dropped, it is dropped among themselves in the most correct view. And we conditioned it to be six, excluding witr, for it is not counted.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement of the text of Maraqi al-Falah on the rules of order (tartib) between missed prayers (fawa'it) and the current prayer (hadirah). The commentary explains three excuses that drop the requirement of order: (1) narrow time (diq al-waqt), (2) forgetfulness (nisyan), and (3) when missed prayers become six or more. For the narrow time excuse, the passage clarifies that the consideration is for the 'preferred time' (al-waqt al-mustahab) according to Imam Muhammad, which is the relied-upon view, overriding the earlier disagreement. The proof is that preserving the completeness of the current prayer (performing it in its earlier, more virtuous time) is prioritized over order, because the obligation to perform the current prayer on time is established by the Qur'an and mutawatir reports, while the order of missed prayers is established by a solitary hadith. When time is tight, observing order would necessitate performing the current prayer in the disliked time (al-waqt al-makruh), thereby reducing its perfection, which is not allowed. The passage also gives a scenario: if someone starts Asr in its earliest time forgetful of a missed Dhuhr, then remembers when the sun has become red (turning Asr into a makruh time), according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf he must cut Asr and pray Dhuhr, but according to Muhammad he continues Asr and makes up Dhuhr after sunset. The second excuse is forgetfulness: if one was genuinely forgetful of the missed prayer at the time of the current prayer, order is dropped because he is excused; the Qur'an says 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.' The third excuse is when the number of missed prayers (real or deemed) reaches six or more; beyond five, the order is dropped to avoid hardship, as indicated by the Qur'anic principle of easing. The six are counted by the exit of the time of the sixth, meaning if one has five missed prayers, the order is still required, but once the sixth current prayer time passes while he still has not made up some, the order between all is dropped. This applies to missed prayers among themselves as well. However, witr is not counted in the six. The passage illustrates the Hanafi method of balancing between primary texts and rational consideration (istihsan).

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tartib): The obligation to perform missed prayers in order according to their appointed times before the current prayer.
الوقت المستحب (al-waqt al-mustahab): The initial, virtuous part of the prayer time, in which performing prayer is most meritorious.
النسيان (al-nisyan): A genuine forgetting of the missed prayer that exempts one from the obligation of order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, what is the correct ruling if a person starts Asr in its earliest time, forgetful of a missed Dhuhr, and then remembers after the sun has turned red (making the remaining Asr time a disliked time)?
A) He must immediately cut Asr and pray Dhuhr, then repeat Asr.
B) He continues Asr and makes up Dhuhr after sunset.
C) He continues Asr, and the missed Dhuhr is automatically forgiven.
D) He completes Asr, then prays Dhuhr in its time if possible, otherwise it is dropped.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires order (tartib) in making up missed prayers, but the obligation is lifted by three excuses: narrow preferred time, forgetfulness, and when missed prayers reach six or more.

Salah — Prayer

6 min
When the Order of Missed Prayers is Dropped: Conditions and Expanded Rulings
Explaining conditions that drop the order of missed prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions that drop the order (tartīb) between missed prayers and the current prayer, state the Hanafi position on the return of order, and apply the ruling to scenarios of multiplicity and forgetfulness.

Core Idea
The order of missed prayers drops due to kathrah (six or more), nisyan (forgetfulness), or time constraints—and does not return.

In the Hanafi school, the order (tartīb) of missed prayers is normally wajib, but the law provides concessions to prevent hardship. Al-Tahtawi's hashya expands on three grounds for dropping the order: when missed prayers become numerous (six or more), when one forgets, or when the current time is too short. He also clarifies the ruling on the return of order and the suspended invalidity (fasād mawqūf) when a missed prayer is remembered during the current prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
Determine that the subject is the obligation of order (tartīb) between missed prayers and the current prayer. The default ruling is that tartīb is wajib.
This obligation is derived from the practice of the Companions and is considered wajib, not fard, because the proof is zanni.
2
Identify the three exceptions that drop the order
From the text, extract the three conditions: (1) multiplicity (kathrah) meaning six or more missed prayers (excluding witr), (2) forgetfulness (nisyān), (3) when performing the current prayer requires excessive extension and time is short.
The third condition is implied from the earlier discussion of prolonging the prayer until the time narrows.
3
Locate the dalīl for the exception
The text cites the Quranic verse 'Allāh does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' and the principle of nafī al-haraj (negation of hardship) as proof for dropping the order.
The dalīl is the Quranic verse 'وما جعل عليكم في الدين من حرج' (Quran 22:78 or 2:185).
4
Trace the ikhtilāf on the return of order
After the order drops due to multiplicity, does it return when the number of missed prayers reduces (e.g., by making up some)? The text mentions two views: al-Tahtawi's (the fatwa) that it does not return, and the view of al-Hidāyah and others that it returns.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi. The fatwa follows the 'asah al-riwāyatain' that the dropped order does not return.
5
Apply the ruling to the case of suspended invalidity
Understand the concept of fasād mawqūf: if one prays a current prayer while remembering a missed one, the current prayer is suspended until the missed one is made up. If the missed prayer is later fulfilled, the current prayer becomes valid.
This is illustrated by the example of praying five prayers while remembering a missed one; if the missed prayer remains owed and then the fifth prayer's time ends, all five become valid.

The book of prayer on missed prayers. And if he prolonged it until the time became narrow, it is not permissible unless he cuts it, then he begins it, even if he began forgetfully and the matter is as is. Then he remembers at the narrowing of time, the timely prayer becomes permissible. And if missed prayers are multiple and the time accommodates some of them along with the timely prayer, then the order falls in the most correct view, as we indicated, because directing to this particular part of the missed prayers is not better than to another, as in al-Fath. (And) the second (forgetfulness) because he is unable to perform; forgetfulness cannot be undertaken; Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity; and because its time has not come into existence due to not remembering it, so it does not coincide with the timely prayer. (And) the third: when the real or legal missed prayers become six, because if order were obligatory in them they would fall into great hardship, which is repelled by the text. And the consideration is the exit of the time of the sixth, in the sound view, because multiplicity is by entering the limit of repetition; and it is narrated by the entry of the time of the sixth because the excess over five is in the ruling of repetition. And an example of legal multiplicity: we will mention it: he prays five prayers remembering a missed prayer without having made it up until the time of the sixth of the performed prayers exits while remembering. And just as the order falls between them and the present prayer, it falls among themselves in the most correct view. And we restricted it to being six (excluding witr, for it does not count as [؟] multiplicity... other until the sun rises; its prescribed time is what follows sunrise and what precedes it is supererogatory... And in al-Mujtaba: if he cannot perform the timely prayer except with shortening recitation and actions, he observes order and confines himself to the minimum with which the prayer is valid. He said: 'And the matter is as is' i.e., he prolonged it until the time became narrow. He said: 'the timely prayer becomes permissible' and he is not obligated to cut because his beginning it initially was permissible, and if he cuts it, he could begin it again, so cutting has no benefit, so remaining is more deserving of permissibility because it is easier than starting. He said: 'as in al-Fath' — what is in al-Fath is a preference for the impermissibility of the timely prayer unless he makes up that part; and it is said: according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) it is permissible; al-Zahidi said it is the most correct and explained it with what the author said. He said: 'and the second: forgetfulness' and ignorance is not considered. The expression of al-Nuqayah: the order is obligatory even if one is ignorant of it. Its commentator al-Allama al-Qahastani said: according to our three imams; and from al-Hasan from him that if he did not know it, it is not obligatory upon him, and the majority took that, as in al-Timirtashi. And what is in al-Zailai: that the considered assumption attaches to forgetfulness, like one who prays Dhuhr remembering the abandonment of the missed prayer, his Dhuhr becomes invalid; then when he makes up Fajr, then prays Asr remembering Dhuhr, his Asr is valid because there is no missed prayer on him in his assumption at the time of performing Asr, and that is a considered assumption because he is a mujtahid in it. So what is meant is the assumption of the mujtahid, for he is not obligated the ijtihad of an imam or an ignorant person who has no specific madhhab: he prays, then remembers, and does not imitate a mujtahid, and does not ask a faqih — his prayer is valid because it coincides with a mujtahid in it. As for the muqallid of Abu Hanifa, his opinion contrary to his imam's madhhab is not considered. And if he is a muqallid of al-Shafi'i, then his prayer is not invalid and does not depend on anything. The author informed in his hashya from al-Bahr. He said: 'because if it were obligatory' etc., and because stipulating order at that time might lead to missing the timely prayer, which is prohibited. He said: 'it is repelled by the text' — Allah says: 'And He has not placed upon you in religion any hardship. Allah intends ease for you and does not intend hardship.' [Quran 2:185]. He said: 'and it is narrated' i.e., from [؟] as a dropper [؟]. The book of prayer: Isha, regarding the multiplicity of missed prayers by consensus. As for according to the two, it is apparent because they say it is sunnah, and because it is a practical fard according to him (Abu Hanifa) and it is part of the complete function of the day and night, and multiplicity is not obtained except by increase over it in terms of times or hours, and witr has no entry in that at all. (And) although its order is obligatory (with Fajr and others) as we explained, the order among the missed prayers that were numerous does not return (by their becoming few) by making up some of them, because what has fallen does not return in the most correct of the two narrations, and the fatwa is upon it, and preferring its return is preference without a preponderant. (And) the order also does not return (by the loss of) a (new) prayer i.e., a new one he left (after) forgetting (six old ones, then he remembers them) in the most correct view in both cases (i.e., the two forms) for what we mentioned, and the fatwa is upon it. Then he branched upon the obligation of order in the original chapter with his saying: (if he prays an obligatory prayer while remembering a missed prayer, even if it) was witr, then his obligatory prayer becomes invalid with a suspended invalidity that may be determined or lifted. He explained it with his saying: (if) he prays five prayers while remembering in all of them that missed prayer, and it remains in his liability until the time of the fifth of what he prayed after the missed prayer exits while remembering it, i.e., the missed prayer, (then all of them are valid) according to Abu Hanifa. This is Muhammad's saying: 'or in terms of hours according to the two shaykhs, and the preference for considering times has preceded.' He said: 'does not return in the most correct of the two narrations' — and some said the order returns, and it is more precautionary in al-Mujtaba, and it is the correct view mentioned by al-Sadr al-Shahid, and likewise said in al-Tajnīs and al-Mazīd and in al-Hidaya, and it is the most apparent because the cause of dropping is multiplicity, and it has ceased. He said: 'preference without a preponderant' — you have known its preponderant, which is the removal of multiplicity, as indicated by al-Sayyid. He said: 'after forgetting six' — he intended by it 'leaving', and if he had expressed it, it would have been better, because when they reach six, the order drops, even if not due to forgetfulness, and because forgetfulness drops the order in the smallest number, as indicated by al-Sayyid. He said: 'then he remembers them' i.e., the new ones, said by al-Sayyid. He said: 'in the most correct of the two views' — and it is said it is not permissible according to some, and the past is made as if it never was as a deterrent, and it was corrected in Ma'arij al-Diraya and in al-Muhit, and the fatwa is upon it. He said: 'and the fatwa is upon it' — its justification is that occupying oneself with this missed prayer is not better than occupying oneself with those missed prayers, and occupying oneself with all leads to missing the obligatory prayer in its time. What they said leads to leniency, not deterrence from it, for if one habitually misses prayer and is overcome by laziness, if a fatwa of impermissibility is given, he will miss another, and so on until he reaches the limit of multiplicity, as indicated by al-Sayyid. He said: 'even if it was witr' because it is a practical fard according to him, so witr is considered in invalidation, and it has no specific time, rather its time is the time of Isha, so it is considered when it is missed, its makeup before the exit of the time of the coming Isha or after. He said: 'it may be determined invalidity' i.e., invalidity is possible; the pronoun is for it, or 'determined' is the subject of 'may be' by treating it as intransitive. He said: 'remembering in all of them that missed prayer' — this dispenses with the author's saying 'remembering it'; he only restricted it with remembering because forgetfulness drops the order, so if he forgets in some and remembers in others, the apparent is that consideration is given to those in which he remembers until the number that drops is reached, and considering five other than the missed prayer is correct, contrary to what the apparent expression of some people suggests of considering six besides it. He said: 'all of them are valid' — 'all' is a nominative emphasis for the implied subject in 'are valid'. He said:
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah is a critical refinement of the rulings on the order (tartib) between missed prayers (fawa'it) and current prayers. The author first states the matn's ruling: if a person prolongs the current prayer until the time is about to expire, the current prayer is invalid unless he cuts it and then re-enters. However, if he begins forgetfully and then remembers when time is short, his current prayer is valid. The discussion then focuses on when the obligatory order between missed and current prayers is dropped. Three conditions are identified: (1) If the current time can only accommodate some of the missed prayers along with the current prayer, the order is dropped in the most correct view. (2) If the person has forgotten the missed prayer, he is not required to observe order due to incapacity and Quranic ease. (3) If the missed prayers become 'many'—defined as six or more (real or legal)—then order is dropped to avoid hardship. The author clarifies that witr is not counted in the six for the purpose of dropping order, as it is a practical fard according to Abu Hanifa but not considered in the multiplicity count. The hashya then debates whether order returns after it has been dropped. The relied-upon view is that the dropped order does not return, even if the number of missed prayers later reduces, because what has fallen cannot return. This is the fatwa, though some later scholars (like the author of al-Hidaya) consider the return as more cautious. The passage also covers the case of suspended invalidity (fasad mawquf): if one prays an obligatory prayer while remembering a missed one, the current prayer is suspended; if he then prays five prayers while still remembering the missed one and it remains pending until after the fifth prayer's time, all five become valid according to Abu Hanifa. The commentary emphasizes the practical fatwa and warns against leniency that could lead to habitual negligence.

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tartīb): The obligation to perform missed prayers in the order they were missed before performing the current prayer, and likewise among the missed prayers themselves.
الفوائت (al-fawā'it): Prayers that were missed (not performed in their prescribed time) and are owed as qada.
الكثرة (al-kathrah): The condition of having six or more missed prayers (real or legal) that causes the dropping of the order.
النسيان (al-nisyān): Forgetfulness regarding the missed prayer, which excuses one from observing the order.
فساد موقوف (fasād mawqūf): Suspended invalidity: a prayer performed while remembering a missed prayer is invalidated only if the missed prayer is later found to be owed; if the missing prayer is eventually made up, the earlier prayer becomes valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this hashya, in which of the following cases is the order (tartib) between missed prayers and the current prayer dropped?
A) If a person has five missed prayers and remembers them all.
B) If a person has six missed prayers, including witr.
C) If a person has six missed prayers excluding witr, and remembers them.
D) If a person has three missed prayers and forgot one of them.

Key Takeaway
The order of missed prayers is wajib but drops due to multiplicity (six+, excluding witr), forgetfulness, or time constraints, and once dropped, it does not return according to the fatwa.

Salah — Prayer

One Prayer Invalidates Five, One Validates Five
Explains the ruling of making up missed prayers when tartib is at stake

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the suspended invalidity of prayers performed while remembering a missed prayer, and apply the concept of fasad mawquf to determine when those prayers become valid or become nafl.

Core Idea
Five prayers remembered while missing one become valid if the missed one is made up after the fifth's time; otherwise they become nafl.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a subtle scenario in the obligation of sequence (tartib) for missed prayers. It introduces the principle that the validity of five consecutive prayers performed while conscious of a missed prayer depends on when the missed prayer is made up. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the Hanafi concept of 'suspended invalidity' (fasād mawqūf) and how the multitude of missed prayers (kathra) triggers a relaxation of the sequence requirement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
The case: a person prays five obligatory prayers while consciously remembering a missed prayer. Each of these prayers is initially in a state of suspended invalidity (fasād mawqūf).
2
Understand the two possible outcomes
If the missed prayer is made up after the time of the fifth prayer expires, the five become valid (sahih). If it is made up before that time, the five lose their obligatory status and become nafl.
This is the core of 'one invalidates five, one validates five'.
3
Explain the rationale via the analogy of advancing zakat
Just as advancing zakat depends on the nisab persisting until the year's end to count as obligatory, the validity of the five prayers depends on the missed prayer being made up after the fifth's time. If made up before, the condition fails and the prayers become voluntary.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) used by the Hanafi school.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the five prayers become absolutely invalid (batil) in all cases, requiring repetition of all six prayers. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa, as followed by Abu Yusuf on the issue of the prayers becoming nafl.
The text states 'وعليه الفتوى'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو صلى فرضاً ذاكراً فائتة ولو كانت وتراً فسد فرضه فساداً موقوفاً فإن صلى خمس صلوات متذكراً في كلها تلك المتروكة وبقيت في ذمته حتى خرج وقت الخامسة مما صلاه بعد المتروكة ذاكراً لها صحت جميعها عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لأن الحكم و هو الصحة العلة، الكثرة يقترنان، والكثرة صفة هذا المجموع لأن الفاسد في حكم المتروك فكانت المتروكات ستاً حكماً، واستندت الصفة إلى أولها فجازت كلها كتعجيل الزكاة يتوقف كونها فرضاً على تمام الحول، وبقاء بعد النصاب، فإذا تم على نمائه كان التعجيل فرضاً، وإلا كان نفلاً فلا تبطل الخمس التي صلاها متذكراً للفائتة بقضاء الفائتة المتروكة بعده أي بعد خروج وقت الخامسة لسقوط الترتيب مستنداً وإن قضى الفائتة المتروكة قبل خروج وقت الخامسة مما صلاه متذكراً لها بطل وصف لا أصل ما صلاه متذكراً للفائتة قبلها أي قبل قضائها ولا يبقى متصفاً بأنه فرض، بل صار الذي صلاه نفلا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وهذه هي التي يقال فيها واحدة تفسد خمساً، وواحدة تصحح خمساً، فالمتروكة تفسد الخمس بقضائها في وقت الخامسة من المؤديات بتقرير الفساد والسادسة من المؤديات تصحح الخمس قبلها عند أبي حنيفة وقالا تفسد تلك الصلوات فساداً باتاً لا يحتمل الصحة بحال، ويلزمه قضاء الست كلها المتروكة، والخمس التي أداها بعدها قبل قضائها وهو ذاكر لها، وما يصليه بعد وإن كان ذاكراً للفائتة لصيرورة الفوائت ستاً.
If he prays a fard prayer while remembering a missed prayer, even if it was witr, his fard becomes invalid with a suspended invalidity. If he prays five prayers, remembering in each of them that missed prayer, and it remains in his responsibility until the time of the fifth of what he prayed after the missed prayer while remembering it exits, then all of them become valid according to Abu Hanifa, because the ruling (validity) and the cause (multitude) are simultaneous, and multitude is a quality of this whole, because the invalid [prayer] is in the ruling of the missed one, so the missed ones become six in effect, and the quality attaches to the first of them, so all are permissible, like the advancement of zakat: its being obligatory depends on the completion of the year and the persistence of the nisab, so if it [the nisab] remains at its increase, the advancement becomes obligatory, otherwise it is voluntary. So the five which he prayed while remembering the missed prayer are not invalidated by making up the missed prayer after it, i.e., after the exit of the time of the fifth, due to the dropping of the sequence (tartib) based on it. And if he makes up the missed prayer before the exit of the time of the fifth of what he prayed while remembering it, the description [of being fard] is invalidated, not the essence, of what he prayed while remembering the missed prayer before it, i.e., before making it up, and it is no longer described as fard, rather what he prayed becomes nafl according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. This is what is said: one invalidates five, and one validates five. The missed prayer invalidates the five by making it up in the time of the fifth of the performed prayers by establishing the invalidity, and the sixth of the performed prayers validates the five before it according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: those prayers become invalid with an absolute invalidity that does not admit validity at all, and he must make up all six missed ones, and the five which he performed after them before making them up while remembering them, and whatever he prays after even if he remembers the missed prayer because the missed prayers become six.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi discusses a specific scenario in the chapter of prayer regarding missed prayers (fawa'it) and the obligation of sequence (tartib). The ruling: if a person performs five obligatory prayers while consciously remembering a missed prayer (even witr, which is considered a fard 'amali according to Abu Hanifa), those five prayers are initially in a state of suspended invalidity (fasad mawquf). If, after completing the fifth prayer and its time has passed, he then makes up the missed prayer, all five become valid. This is because the cause ('illah) for dropping the sequence—the multitude (kathra) of missed prayers—is achieved: the five invalid prayers are treated as missed, making the total missed prayers six, which constitutes kathra. The analogy is given of advancing zakat before the year ends: if the nisab remains until the end, the advance payment counts as obligatory; if not, it becomes voluntary. However, if he makes up the missed prayer before the time of the fifth prayer expires, then the description of being fard is invalidated for the five prayers (but not their essence), and they become nafl (supererogatory) according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. This is the famous principle: 'one invalidates five, one validates five.' The 'one' is the missed prayer made up early (invalidates the description), and the 'one' is the sixth performed prayer (which validates the previous five). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) hold a stricter view: the five prayers become absolutely invalid (batil), requiring repetition of all six prayers. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view, as it considers the subtlety of the cause and the suspended nature of the invalidity.

Key Terms
فائتة (fāʾitah): A missed obligatory prayer that was not performed in its appointed time and must be made up.
ترتيب (tartīb): The obligation to perform missed prayers in the order they were missed before performing current prayers.
فساد موقوف (fasād mawqūf): A suspended invalidity where the prayer is neither fully valid nor fully invalid until a later event determines its status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in this passage, what is the status of five prayers performed while remembering a missed prayer if the missed prayer is made up after the time of the fifth prayer?
A) They become nafl.
B) They become absolutely invalid (batil).
C) They are valid (sahih).
D) They remain in suspended invalidity.

Key Takeaway
The validity of prayers performed while remembering a missed prayer depends on when the missed prayer is made up, due to the concept of suspended invalidity and the condition of multitude (kathra).

Salah — Prayer

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Prayers Performed While Remembering a Missed Prayer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of making up a missed prayer on prayers performed while remembering it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on this issue and explain the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling.

Core Idea
If a man prays five prayers while remembering a missed prayer, the relied-upon view is they become nafl, not invalid.

In this lesson, we explore a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of making up a missed prayer on those performed while its reorder was known. The source from Hashiya al-Tahtawi presents the arguments of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and clarifies the underlying usul principle regarding the intention (tahrimah). By the end, you will understand why the majority position prevails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the question
A person who has missed a fard prayer performs a series of five prayers while remembering that missed prayer. Then later, he makes up that missed prayer. Two timings: makeup before the fifth performed prayer's time ends, or after. What happens to the five performed prayers?
The makeup timing is key.
2
State the positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: If makeup is after the fifth prayer's time, the five become nafl (supererogatory) but remain valid; if before, they lose their obligatory description but stay valid as nafl. Muhammad al-Shaybani: The five prayers become invalid (batil) regardless of timing, and all six (including the missed one) must be repeated.
This is a classic ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the evidence (tahrimah)
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the opening takbir (tahrimah) was contracted for the essence of the prayer with the attribute of obligation. Invalidation of the attribute does not nullify the essence. Muhammad argues that the tahrimah was for the obligatory prayer itself, so when the obligatory nature is invalidated, the entire prayer is invalid.
This is a deconstruction of the intention.
4
Consider the analogy with zakat
The author draws a parallel to advance payment of zakat: if the nisab is completed by year's end, the payment becomes obligatory (fard); otherwise it is considered nafl. Similarly, the validity of the performed prayers depends on the timing of the makeup – a condition that becomes clear later.
Qiyas shows that suspension of a ruling is not unprecedented.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: the performed prayers become nafl, not invalid. This is supported by the majority of later Hanafi authorities.
The author of this hashia emphasizes this as the more sound view.

رمضانين) وأما إذا كان من رمضان وأحد فلا يحتاج إلى التعيين إتفاقاً حتى لو كان
May Allah have mercy on him because the ruling, which is validity, and the cause, the multiplicity, are connected, and multiplicity is an attribute of this total because the invalid is in the ruling of the omitted, so the omitted become six in ruling, and the attribute is attached to the first of them, so all are permissible like the advance payment of zakat, its being obligatory depends on the completion of the year and the remaining of part of the nisab, so if it is completed with its growth, the advance payment is obligatory, otherwise it is supererogatory. (So the five prayers that he prayed while remembering the missed one are not invalidated) by the makeup of the missed prayer after the time of the fifth has exited, due to the lapse of order based on that. And if he makes up the missed prayer before the exit of the time of the fifth, the description (not the essence) of what he prayed while remembering it is invalidated, and it no longer remains described as obligatory, but rather what he prayed becomes supererogatory according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. This is what is said: one invalidates five, and one validates five. So the missed prayer invalidates the five by its makeup in the time of the fifth of the performed prayers, by confirming the invalidity, and the sixth of the performed prayers validates the five before it. That is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: those prayers become invalid with absolute invalidity that does not admit validity under any condition, and it becomes necessary for him to make up all six: the missed one, the five that he performed after it before making it up while remembering it, and whatever he prays after while remembering the missed one, because the missed ones become six. His statement: (and multiplicity) meaning multiplicity of missed prayers, correct. And when it was objected that the missed prayer is only one and the five are performed, he answered by saying: because the invalid etc. His statement: and the attribute (multiplicity) is attached. His statement: (so all are permissible) because the order lapses from the first prayer he left, due to the necessity of establishing the ruling based on it, to be attributed to multiplicity which is the cause, not the last which is not a cause. His statement: (like the advance payment of zakat) he indicated by it that the suspension of a ruling on a matter until its state becomes clear is not unprecedented, like the suspension of zakat, and the suspension of the Maghrib prayer performed on the way to Muzdalifah: if he repeats it before dawn, its obligatory nature is invalidated, otherwise not; and the validity of the prayer of the excused person if the excuse ceases after it depends on his repeating it in the second time: if he repeats it, it is valid, otherwise not, as stated in the sharh. His statement: (and the remaining of part of the nisab) meaning during the year, but at its end, completion is necessary. His statement: (the advance payment is obligatory) meaning the advanced amount is obligatory. His statement: (according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) because the tahrimah was contracted for the essence of the prayer with the attribute of obligation, so the invalidation of the attribute does not necessitate the invalidation of the essence; according to Muhammad, the essence is invalidated because the tahrimah was contracted for the obligatory prayer, so when the obligatory nature is invalidated, the tahrimah is also invalidated. And know that Abu Yusuf agreed with the Imam in not invalidating the essence of the prayer if he makes up the missed prayer before the lapse of the five, but disagreed with him regarding the suspension of its validity on delaying the makeup of the missed prayer until after the lapse of the five, so he said: its obligatory nature is not valid even if he delays it after its lapse. His statement: (by confirming the invalidity) i.e., by confirming the suspended invalidity, it is an idafah of the masdar to its maf'ul, and the prepositional phrase relates to his statement 'invalidates'. His statement: (and the sixth of the performed prayers etc.) he brought that as a response to what occurs in most books that the conversion of all to permissibility is permissible and dependent on performing six prayers after the missed one, but the intended meaning is only to emphasize the exit of the time of the fifth of the performed prayers, not the condition of the sixth, nor even the entry of its time, because the exit of a time does not necessitate the entry of another, as if the fifth of the performed prayers is Subh and the sun rises. His statement: (but since the exit necessarily implies the entry of a time) the implication is denied as you have recently learned, except if it is said: the implication exists in most times, so the general is considered. His statement: (and its performance in it generally) if his word 'generally' is linked to entry and performance, the previous answer results. His statement: (in place of that) i.e., the exit of the time of the fifth. His statement: (and when the missed prayers become numerous) the intended meaning is absolute numerousness even if it does not cancel the order, as stated in the sharh. His statement: (the overlapping of obligations and times) which are causes, so the causes differ as the effects differ. His statement: (like his saying: I pray the Zuhr of Monday etc.) there is a subtlety in it, which is drawing attention to the date of composition of this section, thus the author noted it, and in the sharh: Zuhr of Thursday, tenth of Dhul Hijjah, year 1045, so between the two dates is eight years, four months, and eighteen days. His statement: (and it is the most correct) he preferred it in al-Khaniya and al-Khulasa, and the author of al-Fath followed it. His statement: (so let him refer to al-Kanz) i.e., let the one afflicted by the incident refer to the ruling mentioned in al-Kanz, and the lam in l'ilkanz means 'to', as Allah said: 'Return to them that they do not return to them', and his statement: 'for it is vast' i.e., the ruling in it is spacious, and it contains an indication of the scope of al-Kanz over this work, and in a copy: 'for it was vast' in past tense. His statement: (and Allah is kind and merciful) i.e., extremely merciful, so out of His mercy He did not burden this ummah with hardship in matters, rather He said: 'Allah intends for you ease and does not intend for you hardship' [2:185], and what is more fitting for ease and kindness is what is in al-Kanz, and He is All-Knowing of what is upon him, so He causes it to drop from him, therefore He said: 'Vast, All-Knowing.' His statement: (of two Ramadans) and as for if it is from one Ramadan, then there is no need for specification by agreement, even if it were...
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi addresses a complex scenario in Hanafi fiqh regarding missed prayers (fawa'it). The ruling concerns a person who, while remembering a missed prayer, performs five prayers. The question is the effect of subsequently making up that missed prayer. According to the base text, if the person makes up the missed prayer after the time of the fifth performed prayer has exited, the five performed prayers are not invalidated (they become nafl). However, if the missed prayer is made up before the exit of the fifth prayer's time, the performed prayers lose their description as obligatory (fard) but their essence remains valid, becoming nafl. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad holds that the performed prayers are invalidated entirely and must be repeated. The passage then discusses the reasoning: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the initial intention (tahrimah) was for the essence of the prayer with the attribute of obligation; the invalidation of the attribute does not invalidate the essence. Muhammad argues that the tahrimah was for the obligatory prayer itself, so its invalidity invalidates the entire prayer. The author then clarifies that Abu Yusuf differed from Abu Hanifa only regarding the suspension of validity on delaying the makeup until after the five prayers. The commentary also draws an analogy with advance payment of zakat: if the nisab is completed, the advance payment becomes obligatory; otherwise it is nafl. Similarly, the validity of the performed prayers depends on the timing of the makeup. Later, the passage shifts to the issue of specifying which missed prayer one is making up when there are many from different days. It states that if the missed prayers are numerous, one must specify by intending the first or last missed prayer. This is to avoid confusion due to overlapping obligations and times. The author notes that this is the most correct view, though some texts say no specification is needed. The passage ends with a similar ruling for fasting: when making up fasts from two different Ramadans, specification is also required according to one correction, though another correction says it is not needed.

Key Terms
فائتة (fā'ita): A missed obligatory prayer that was not performed within its prescribed time.
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory prayer, voluntary, not obligatory, but rewarded if performed.
تحريمة (taḥrīma): The opening takbir that initiates the prayer and establishes its sanctity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what happens to five prayers performed while remembering a missed prayer, if the missed prayer is made up after the time of the fifth performed prayer has exited?
A) They become invalid and must be repeated.
B) They become nafl (supererogatory) and are valid.
C) They remain fard (obligatory) as originally intended.
D) They become makruh but still valid.

Key Takeaway
When makeup of a missed prayer occurs after the fifth prayer's time, the performed prayers become nafl, not invalid, based on the principle that the intention (tahrimah) is for the essence, not the obligation.

Specification When Making Up Many Missed Prayers
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether ta'yin is required for qada of many missed prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether specification (ta'yin) is required when making up many missed prayers and apply the preferred ruling that no specification is needed.

Core Idea
When prayers are many, specification is not required—ease is the principle.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a practical issue for someone with many missed prayers: must they specify each one? The matn initially suggests hardship, but the commentary resolves that the more correct Hanafi view is that no such specification is needed, easing the burden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling and hardship
The matn states that when missed prayers are many, one must specify each prayer (e.g., by date). This causes difficulty.
The example given is a precise verbal specification.
2
Understand the easing options
To ease the burden, one may intend either the first or the last missed prayer of that type. This achieves specification implicitly.
The text offers two methods: intending the first or the last.
3
Recognize the conflicting view from al-Kanz
Al-Kanz and al-Qunyah hold that specification is not needed at all; one may simply intend the type of prayer (e.g., Zuhr).
The commentary states this is the more correct (al-asaḥḥ) opinion.
4
Note the correction of al-Zayla'i and the resolution
Al-Zayla'i corrected al-Kanz, requiring specification. However, the hashiya advises that the matter is broadened by differing corrections and one may follow al-Kanz for ease.
The preferred position is no specification.
5
State the mufta bihi
Operative ruling: specification is not required when making up many missed prayers; intending the type suffices.
This is the view adopted in later fatwa works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كثرت الفوائت يحتاج لتعيين كل صلاة يقضيها التزاحم الفروض والأوقات كقوله : أصلي ظهر الإثنين ثامن عشر جمادى الثانية سنة أربع وخمسين وألف وهذا فيه كلفة فإذا أراد تسهيل الأمر عليه نوى أول ظهر عليه أدرك وقته ولم يصله فإذا نواه كذلك فيما يصليه يصير أولا، فيصح بمثل ذلك، وهكذا أو إن شاء نوى آخره فيقول أصلي آخر ظهر أدركته، ولم أصله بعد فإذا فعل كذلك فيما يليه يصير آخراً بالنظر لما قبله فيحصل التعيين ويخالف هذا ما قاله في الكنز في مسائل شتى أنه لا يحتاج للتعيين، وهو الأصح على ما قاله في القنية من يقضي ليس عليه ينوي أول صلاة كذا أو آخر فينوي ظهر أعلى أو عصراً، أو نحوهما على الأصح انتهى وإن خالفه تصحيح الزيلعي فقد اتسع الأمر باختلاف التصحيح فليرجع للكنز فإنه واسع والله رؤوف رحيم واسع عليم
And when missed prayers become many, he needs to specify each prayer he makes up due to the crowding of obligations and times, such as saying: 'I pray the Zuhr of Monday, the 18th of Jumada al-Thani, year 1054 AH,' and this involves hardship. So if he wants to make it easy for himself, he intends the first Zuhr that was due upon him—its time came and he did not pray it. So if he intends thus for what he prays, it becomes the first, and it is valid with that, and so on. Or if he wishes, he intends the last, saying: 'I pray the last Zuhr that I perceived and did not yet pray.' So if he does that for the next one, it becomes the last relative to the previous one, and specification is achieved. This contradicts what was said in al-Kanz in various matters, that he does not need specification, and that is the more correct opinion according to what was said in al-Qunyah: 'Whoever makes up missed prayers does not have to intend the first or last prayer; rather, he may intend Zuhr or 'Asr or similar, on the more correct opinion.' So concludes. And even if the correction of al-Zayla'i contradicts it, the matter is broadened by differing corrections, so let him refer to al-Kanz, for it is comprehensive. And Allah is Kind, Merciful, All-Encompassing, All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage discusses the need for specification (ta'yin) when making up many missed prayers. The matn states that when missed prayers become numerous, one must specify which prayer is being performed because of the conflicting obligations and times. The example given is specifying the exact date and time. However, to ease hardship, one may intend either the first or the last of the missed prayers. The commentary notes that al-Kanz holds that specification is not needed, and that this is the more correct opinion according to al-Qunyah. Despite the correction of al-Zayla'i requiring specification, the hashiya advises referring to al-Kanz for ease. The discussion is within the Hanafi school, showing that there is room for differing corrections.

Key Terms
تعيين (ta'yin): Specification of which particular prayer (e.g., first or last missed) one is performing when making up missed prayers.
فوائت (fawa'it): Plural of fā'itah; missed obligatory prayers that must be made up.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the more correct opinion say about the need for specification when making up many missed prayers?
A) Must specify exactly which prayer (e.g., 'Zuhr of Monday')
B) Must specify first or last of the missed prayers
C) Does not need specification; can just intend the type of prayer
D) Must not pray until the exact number is known

Key Takeaway
When making up many missed prayers, the preferred Hanafi view is that no specification is needed; intention to perform the type (e.g., Zuhr) suffices.

Excuse of Ignorance for a New Muslim in Dar al-Harb
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school excuses a new Muslim in dar al-harb for missed acts of worship and refute the counter-argument of Imam Zafar.

Core Idea
Obligation requires knowledge; ignorance of the details of worship excuses a new Muslim in dar al-harb.

This passage addresses a key fiqh question: is a person who embraces Islam in non-Muslim territory liable for missed acts of worship before they learned of the obligations? The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa, holds that such a person is excused because the divine address (khitab) only binds one who has knowledge of it or its evidence. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the rational proof, and the intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zafar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the person and the acts
The passage concerns a person who becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and, due to ignorance, misses prayer, fasting, and zakat. Identify the specific acts mentioned.
The acts are salah, sawm, and zakat.
2
State the general Hanafi principle
The key legal principle is that obligation (khitab) is contingent upon knowledge of the command or its evidence. Without such knowledge, the person is not held accountable.
This is a usul al-fiqh principle applied here.
3
Apply the principle to dar al-harb
In dar al-harb, the evidence for the details of worship (e.g., that prayer is obligatory) may not be available. Therefore, the new Muslim is excused for the period of ignorance.
Contrast with dar al-Islam where evidence is present.
4
Contrast with dar al-Islam
A Muslim in dar al-Islam is not excused because the evidence for obligations is accessible. The ruling is conditional on the availability of evidence.
This highlights the role of environment in legal obligation.
5
Examine Zafar's objection and Hanafi response
Imam Zafar analogized to belief in God, which is rationally evident and cannot be excused. The Hanafi response distinguishes between self-evident truths (existence of God) and detailed rulings (prayer, etc.) that require transmitted knowledge.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the person must make up missed acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعذر من أسلم بدار الحرب فلم يصم ولم يصل، ولم يزك وهكذا بجهله الشرائع أي الأحكام المشروعات مدة جهله لأن الخطاب إنما يلزم بالعلم به أو بدليله، ولم يوجد بخلاف المسلم بدار الإسلام وألزمه زفر بها كما يلزمه الإيمان قلنا دليل وجود الصانع ظاهر عقلاً فلا يعذر بجهله ولا دليل عنده على وجود فرض الصلاه ونحوها فيعذر به .
And one who becomes Muslim in dar al-harb is excused if he did not fast, pray, or give zakat due to his ignorance of the laws (shara'i')—i.e., the prescribed rulings—for the duration of his ignorance, because the address (khitab) is only obligatory upon knowledge of it or its evidence, and that was not present. This is unlike a Muslim in dar al-Islam; Zafar obliged him with them just as he is obliged with belief. We say: the evidence for the existence of the Creator is rationally apparent, so he is not excused by ignorance, and there is no evidence for him regarding the obligation of prayer and similar acts, so he is excused thereby.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage establishes that a person who accepts Islam in dar al-harb (non-Muslim territory) is not held accountable for missed acts of worship if he was ignorant of the obligations. The reasoning is that obligation (khitab) is contingent upon knowledge of the command or its evidence. In dar al-harb, the evidence for prayer etc. may not be available, so the person is excused. In contrast, a Muslim in dar al-Islam is expected to know, as evidence is present. Zafar disagreed and equated it to belief in God, but the author refutes by stating that belief in the Creator is rationally evident, whereas the details of worship are not.

Key Terms
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory not under Islamic rule where Islam is not dominant.
الشرائع (al-shara'i'): The prescribed laws and obligations of Islam, such as prayer, fasting, zakat.
الخطاب (al-khitab): The divine address or command that imposes an obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a new Muslim in dar al-harb excused for missed prayers according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because he did not have access to water for wudu
B) Because the obligation to pray only applies after he learns of it
C) Because he was forced to live there
D) Because he was traveling

Key Takeaway
A new Muslim in dar al-harb is not required to make up missed acts of worship due to ignorance, because obligation is contingent upon knowledge.

Making Up Missed Prayers When the Number Is Unknown
Rulings on unknown missed prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for making up missed prayers when the exact number is unknown, including the use of precautionary methods and handling doubts.

Core Idea
When uncertain of missed prayers, pray until certainty; use flexible methods like four-rak'ah prayers to cover possible obligations.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi provides crucial guidance for someone who cannot recall the exact number of missed obligatory prayers (fawa'it). The text outlines a step-by-step approach: first using best judgment, then performing prayers until certainty. It also discusses precautionary methods to ensure no obligation is left unfulfilled, including the permissibility of making up prayers never missed as a precaution. The lesson will cover these rulings and also address doubts about whether a prayer was performed.

Guided Path
1
Determine the procedure when quantity unknown
Identify the initial step: act upon one's best opinion (ra'y). If no opinion, pray repeatedly until certain that no missed prayer remains.
This is the foundational principle for dealing with unknown quantities of missed prayers.
2
Understand the precautionary method
Examine the ruling on making up prayers one never missed as a precaution, and learn the method of praying a four-rak'ah prayer that can serve as both qada for witr or as nafl, and praying maghrib as four rak'ahs with three sittings.
This method ensures coverage of possible obligations without changing the nature of the prayer.
3
Prioritize making up missed prayers
Note that qada of missed prayers takes precedence over most supererogatory prayers, except for emphasized sunnah, duha, and tasbih prayers.
This prioritization is based on the importance of fulfilling obligations.
4
Handle doubts about a missed prayer
Learn the ruling for doubting whether a prayer was performed: if doubt occurs within the time, repeat; if after time, assume it was performed. Also, if doubt occurs during a prayer about a rak'ah deficiency, complete with extra sitting if still in prayer, otherwise ignore.
The default assumption is that a Muslim performs his obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة : من لا يدري كمية الفوائت يعمل بأكبر رأيه فإن لم يكن له رأي يقض حتى يتيقن أنه لم يبق عليه شيء ومن قضى صلاة عمره مع أنه لم يفته شيء منها احتياطاً قيل : يكره، وقيل : لا لأن كثيراً من السلف قد فعل ذلك لكن لا يقضي في وقت تكره فيه النافلة، والأفضل أن يقرأ في الأخيرتين السورة مع الفاتحة لأنها نوافل من وجه فلأن يقرأ الفاتحة، والسورة في أربع الفرض على احتماله أولى من أن يدع الواجب في النفل، ويقنت في الوتر، ويقعد قدر التشهد في ثالثته، ثم يصلي ركعة رابعة، فإن كان وتراً فقد أداه وإن لم يكن فقد صلى التطوع أربعاً، ولا يضره القعود، وكذا يصلي المغرب أربعاً بثلاث قعدات والاشتغال بقضاء الفوائت أولى، وأهم. من النوافل إلا السنة المعروفة وصلاة الضحى، وصلاة التسبيح، والصلاة التي وردت في الأخبار فتلك بنية النفل وغيرها بنية القضاء كذا في المضمرات عن الظهيرية، وفتاوى الحجة، ومراده بالسنة المعروفة المؤكدة، وقوله : وغيرها بنية القضاء مراده به أن ينوي القضاء إذا أراد فعل غير ما ذكر فإنه الأولى، بل المتعين، ولو شك أنه صلى أم لا والوقت باق أعاد لأن سبب الوجوب قائم والأداء فيه شك، وإن خرج الوقت ثم شك فلا شيء عليه، لأن قد فات، الوجوب : سبب الأداء فيه شك أي والظاهر من حال المسلم أداء الصلاة في وعدم وقتها، وفيه تأمل، وإن شك في نقصان الصلاة أنه ترك ركعة، أم لا فإن لم يفرغ من | فعليه إتمامها، ويقعد في كل ركعة وإن شك بعدما فرغ لا شيء عليه كذا في البحر والله سبحان وتعالى أعلم، وأستغفر الله العظيم.
Conclusion: One who does not know the quantity of missed prayers acts upon his best opinion. If he has no opinion, he prays until he is certain that nothing remains upon him. And whoever makes up prayers of his entire life although he missed nothing, as a precaution, it is said it is disliked, and it is said no, because many of the salaf did that. But he should not pray in a time when supererogatory prayer is disliked. It is better to recite a surah after al-Fatihah in the last two rak'ahs because they are supererogatory in some respect, so reciting al-Fatihah and a surah in four rak'ahs of an obligatory prayer, based on probability, is better than abandoning a wajib in supererogatory prayer. He also performs qunut in witr, sits for the tashahhud in the third, then prays a fourth rak'ah. If it was witr, he has performed it; if not, then he has performed a four-rak'ah voluntary prayer, and the sitting does not harm him. Likewise, he prays maghrib as four rak'ahs with three sittings. Occupying oneself with making up missed prayers is more important than supererogatory prayers except the known sunnah, duha prayer, tasbih prayer, and prayers mentioned in reports—those are performed with the intention of supererogatory, while others with the intention of making up. This is from al-Mudmarat and al-Zahiriyyah and Fatawa al-Hujjah. His intent with 'known sunnah' is the emphasized sunnah. And his saying 'others with the intention of making up' means that if one intends to do other than what was mentioned, he should intend making up, as that is better, indeed required. And if he doubts whether he has prayed or not while the time remains, he repeats because the cause of obligation is present and performing is in doubt. If the time has passed and then he doubts, nothing is upon him, because the obligation has passed; the cause of performing is in doubt. The apparent state of a Muslim is that he performed the prayer in its time, but there is consideration. If he doubts a deficiency in the prayer, like whether he left a rak'ah or not, if he has not yet finished, he must complete it and sit in every rak'ah. If he doubts after finishing, nothing is upon him. This is from al-Bahr. And Allah knows best. And I seek forgiveness from Allah the Great.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This conclusion provides guidance for someone who does not recall the exact number of missed prayers. He should use his best judgment; if still unsure, he should continue praying until certain that no obligations remain. The passage also discusses the permissibility of making up prayers one never missed as a precaution, with a difference of opinion. It then lists various details about how to pray when unsure, including recitation, qunut, and handling doubt. The key principle is that when in doubt, one should base actions on the most likely scenario. The text also prioritizes making up missed prayers over supererogatory acts, except for certain emphasized sunnah prayers.

Key Terms
الفوائت (al-fawa'it): Missed obligatory prayers that need to be made up.
قضاء (qada): Making up an obligatory act after its prescribed time has passed.
أكبر الرأي (akbar al-ra'y): Best opinion or reasonable estimate.
السنة المعروفة (al-sunnah al-ma'rufa): The emphasized sunnah (rawatib) prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Repeat Fajr immediately.
B) Assume he performed it and do nothing.
C) Make it up later as qada.
D) He is required to repeat it at the same time the next day.

Key Takeaway
When unsure of the number of missed prayers, rely on best judgment or pray until certain; and use flexible methods like four-rak'ah prayers to cover possible obligations, while giving qada priority over most voluntary prayers.

Excuse for Ignorance in Dar al-Harb and Qada of Fasts
Commentary on rulings for converts in Dar al-Harb and making up missed fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a convert in Dar al-Harb is excused for missing acts other than iman, identify the rational proof, and apply the qada rule for missed fasts without specifying the exact days.

Core Idea
A convert in Dar al-Harb is excused for missed acts; only iman is immediately required.

This lesson examines two related fiqh issues from the Hanafi school: the excuse for a new convert in non-Muslim territory who neglects fundamental acts like prayer and fasting due to ignorance, and the details of making up missed fasts when the number is known but specific days are not specified. The first issue involves a dispute with other schools, while the second clarifies the validity of a general qada intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Read the opening sentence: a person who converts in Dar al-Harb is excused for missing prayers, fasts, and zakat during his ignorance. Note the exception: iman (belief) is not excused.
The text says 'يعذر' (he is excused) – this is the Hanafi position.
2
Examine the dispute (ikhtilaf)
Zufar (and al-Shafi‘i, Ahmad) opposed, arguing the convert is liable just as he is for iman. The Hanafi rebuttal distinguishes between rationally evident obligations (iman) and those dependent on revelation (far‘i acts).
The proof is rational: 'دليل وجود الصانع ظاهر عقلاً' – evidence for the Creator is rationally apparent.
3
Apply the qada rule for fasts
If someone owes two missed days from one Ramadan and makes up only one day without specifying which, it is valid. The reason: the cause (the month) is one; the obligation is to complete the number. The same applies if the days are from two different Ramadans according to the chosen view.
Intention of qada in general suffices; no need to specify the exact date.
4
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Hanafi view, the convert has no obligation to make up missed acts before learning. Under the opposing view, he must make them up and is sinful for delaying.
This affects converts today who may have lived years as Muslims without knowledge.

If the time has passed, then he doubts, there is nothing on him because the obligation has passed—the cause: the performance is doubtful—and the apparent state of a Muslim is that he performed the prayer. There is consideration in this. If he doubts a deficiency in prayer, whether he left a rak‘ah or not, if he has not yet finished, he must complete it and sit in every rak‘ah; if he doubts after finishing, there is nothing on him. Thus it is in al-Bahr. And Allah the Glorified and Exalted knows best, and I seek forgiveness from Allah the Great.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on Maraqi al-Falah covering several rulings. It begins by addressing the excuse for a person who converts to Islam in Dar al-Harb (non-Muslim territory) and neglects the fundamental acts of worship—prayer, fasting, zakat—due to ignorance of their obligation. The Hanafi position states he is excused because the divine address (khitab) only becomes binding upon knowledge of the ruling or its evidence (such as living in Dar al-Islam where he would be expected to learn). Since the convert in Dar al-Harb lacks such knowledge or means, he is excused. This stands in contrast to the opinion of Zufar (and al-Shafi‘i and Ahmad), who argued that he is liable just as he is liable for iman. The Hanafi rejoinder explains that the existence of the Creator is rationally evident and thus ignorance of tawhid is inexcusable, but the obligation of specific practices like prayer is not rationally deducible, hence excusable. The next part discusses qada of fasts: if a person owes two missed days from the same Ramadan and makes up only one day without specifying which, it is valid, as the cause (the month) is singular. The text elaborates on intention when making up multiple days. Then a 'Khatimah' (conclusion) provides practical guidance for one who cannot recall the exact number of missed prayers: he should act on his best estimate, and if he has no basis, he must pray until certain that no missed prayer remains. The commentary then addresses the ruling of praying one's entire life's prayers as a precaution (even if nothing missed) and discusses recommended recitations and actions in qada prayers. Finally, it covers doubts in prayer—both before and after the time—and whether repeats are required.

Key Terms
دار الحرب (Dar al-Harb): Territory where non-Muslims rule and Islamic law is not enforced; a convert there may lack access to religious knowledge.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed obligatory act of worship after its prescribed time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the convert did not intend to commit sin
B) Because iman is rationally evident while far‘i acts are not
C) Because Dar al-Harb is a place of war and obligations are suspended
D) Because the convert was physically prevented from performing acts

Key Takeaway
A new Muslim in Dar al-Harb is excused for missing acts due to ignorance; iman alone is immediately obligatory.

Cutting a Solo Prayer to Join the Congregation
Ruling on cutting a solo prayer to join the congregation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting a fard prayer to join the congregation and identify the conditions under which it is permissible.

Core Idea
Cutting a fard prayer for the same congregation is completion, not invalidation.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: when a person starts a prayer alone and then a congregation for the same prayer is established, may he cut his prayer to join? The ruling hinges on the distinction between form and meaning—outward deficiency can be inner completion. We will explore the conditions, proof, and practical application of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act under discussion is cutting (qaṭ‘) a prayer that one has already begun alone, in order to join the congregation (jamā‘ah). The Hanafi school rules this permissible (jā‘iz) under certain conditions.
The ruling is not general; it applies only when the congregation is for the same fard or its qada.
2
Understand the Underlying Principle
The principle is that 'deficiency for the purpose of completion is itself completion.' Outwardly, cutting a prayer seems like a deficiency, but in meaning it perfects the act by allowing the worshiper to gain the reward of congregation.
This is supported by analogies (qiyas) like demolishing a mosque to rebuild it or adjusting a prostration due to an obstacle.
3
Recognize the Proof (Dalīl)
The proof is qiyas: just as demolishing a mosque for renewal is not destruction but improvement, and adjusting a prostration due to a thorn is not deficiency but necessary adaptation, cutting the prayer for congregation is completion. The consideration of meanings takes precedence over forms.
The dalīl is based on the principle of istihsān (juristic preference), as the outward form is overridden by the inner purpose.
4
Apply the Conditions
The cutting is permissible only if: (1) the solo prayer is a fard (adā‘ or qada) or a vowed prayer (nadhr) for which the congregation is formed, (2) the congregation is established in the same place (maḥall al-adā‘), and (3) the congregation is for the same prayer (not a different obligation).
For supererogatory (nafl) prayers, cutting is allowed only for a funeral prayer (janāzah) that may be missed.
5
Distinguish Cases Where Cutting is Not Allowed
Cutting is not allowed if: the congregation is in a different location, if the solo prayer is for a different fard or different vowed prayer, or if the solo prayer is nafl without a funeral. In such cases, cutting would be pure nullification (ibṭāl).
The commentary emphasizes that for qada of a different obligation, cutting is not completion and thus prohibited.
